The Intelligent Investor: Why Wait Three Days To Collect Your Cash?
The Intelligent Investor: Why Wait Three Days To Collect Your Cash?
By Jason Zweig
884 words
16 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
By the time you finish reading this sentence, an electronic high-frequency trader can send out thousands of
buy or sell orders. Why, then, does it take up to three days -- and occasionally far longer -- for investors to
receive cash when they sell securities and when they buy?
Consider the saga of Michael Hartner, a retired public-school superintendent who lives near Poughkeepsie,
N.Y. Last Dec. 18, he called three firms where he had retirement accounts to begin transferring the balances
into an Individual Retirement Account rollover at his discount broker. Not until Feb. 16 did Mr. Hartner receive
the last of the proceeds -- although it was $8,000 less than he had been told to expect.
Under Securities and Exchange Commission rules, stock trades must "settle" -- with the buyer delivering cash
and the seller handing over the securities -- no later than three business days afterward. By September 2017,
they should settle within two days after the trade. Fortunately, in the meantime, you don't have to settle for
snail-like delivery.
Nor was it always this way.
Until 1892, messenger boys used to scramble through the streets of lower Manhattan lugging sacks of gold,
bank checks and stock and bond certificates. When the settlement time of 2:15 p.m. approached, pedestrians
risked life and limb as swarms of delivery boys plowed their way toward the cashier windows at the biggest
banks. Without a single computer or cellphone, thousands of stock and bond trades settled one day after they
were made.
Overnight settlement was standard practice until the 1930s, says Bernard McSherry, interim dean of the
NJCU School of Business in Jersey City, N.J., who worked as a broker on the floor of the New York Stock
Exchange for four decades through 2005. The settlement period lengthened to two days in 1938 and three in
1946. Then, in 1968, with average daily volume swelling above 10 million shares and Wall Street drowning in
paperwork, trades began settling on a five-day lag -- where the limit stayed until the SEC lowered it to three
days in 1995.
Almost no one holds paper certificates anymore, with nearly every investment in electronic form, so the risk of
another 1968-scale back-office crisis has waned.
Still, the U.S. stock market trades fast, but settles slowly. Many European stock markets already have moved
to two-day settlement. Stock options have long settled one day after the trade, and China's stock markets
require same-day settlement.
Progress has been slow primarily because of inertia and the intrinsic complexity of a system that handles
billions of trades and trillions of dollars a day.
What's more, brokerage firms long made buckets of money "playing the float," or holding onto customers'
cash during the settlement period, and by collecting fees on securities they lent out for a couple days before
having to deliver them. Today, with interest rates near zero, an extra day's income on billions of dollars in
unsettled assets isn't the bonanza it once was.
A large group of financial firms and other market participants is inching the industry toward Sept. 5, 2017.
That is the target date on which U.S. stock trades finally should have to settle within two days -- the same
standard they had to meet in 1938.
"The benefits to the system far outweigh" any revenue that firms might get by hanging onto securities longer,
says Thomas Price, a managing director at the Securities Industry and Financial Markets Association, a Wall
Street group. He doesn't think playing the float has been a factor.

--- Page 186 ---
Page 186 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Risk is lessened for brokers and investors alike when money remains in limbo for less time, says John Abel,
an executive director at Depository Trust & Clearing Corp., the industry-owned organization that handled
more than 345 million trade settlements last year.
Although mutual-fund companies have up to seven days to release your cash, about 80% of fund sales settle
the day after the transaction, says Marty Burns, chief industry operations officer at the Investment Company
Institute, a trade group for fund managers.
If you do need your money sooner, it pays to ask. Brokerage firms say customers almost never request
settlement sooner than three days after a trade. However, firms will honor those requests. Fidelity
Investments, for example, permits its discount-brokerage customers to ask for next-day settlement -- meaning
that if you sell a stock before 4 p.m. on Monday, you will get the cash proceeds in your account on Tuesday.
Such a trade carries the same commission, $32.95, as any other broker-assisted stock transaction at the firm.
As for Mr. Hartner, clerical error seems to have accounted for much of the delay. Representatives of the firms
that formerly held his retirement accounts -- American Funds, Nationwide Mutual Insurance Co. and
Northwestern Mutual Life Insurance Co. -- say they always strive to release the proceeds of sales as soon as
possible. Spokesman Ryan Ankrom says Nationwide will make up the $8,000 that Mr. Hartner lost when the
stock market crashed in January before the firm could finish processing his sale request. All's well that ends
well, even if it ends later than it should have.

Financial shares in the S&P 500 rose for a fifth consecutive session, while major U.S. indexes crept higher.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average inched up to its highest level since July 20, a day after a bank-led rally
gave the blue-chip index its biggest gain in a month.
The moves came after investors got a snapshot of earnings from some large U.S. banks, boosting a sector
that remains the worst performer in the S&P 500 so far this year.
"When you get sectors that have been underperforming catching up, that speaks to the overall breadth of the
market," said Rob Bernstone, managing director in equity trading at Credit Suisse.
The Dow industrials edged up 18.15 points, or 0.1% to 17926.43. The S&P 500 rose 0.36, or less than 0.1%,
to 2082.78, while the Nasdaq Composite Index fell 1.53, or less than 0.1%, to 4945.89.
Bank of America reported a fall in first-quarter profit, hurt by declining trading revenue and low interest rates.
Wells Fargo said its profit fell as it grappled with a slump in oil prices. Earnings per share for both banks
came in above analysts' expectations.
Financial shares in the S&P 500 rose 0.3%. Bank of America rose 35 cents, or 2.5% to $14.14, rising 10%
over five sessions. Wells Fargo fell 24 cents, or 0.5% to 48.79. J.P. Morgan Chase gained 80 cents, or 1.3%,
to 62.59, a day after better-than-expected results from the bank spurred the rally in financial shares.
"The bar was lowered so much going into the quarter that we're stepping over it, not jumping over it," said
William Delwiche, investment strategist at Robert W. Baird.
Airline stocks were among the biggest gainers in the S&P 500. Delta Air Lines rose 45 cents, or 0.9%, to
48.49 after the company reported its 12th consecutive record quarterly profit. American Airlines rose 1.23, or
3.1%, to 41.17. United Continental Holdings climbed 1.15, or 2.1%, to 56.73 and Southwest Airlines gained
87 cents, or 1.9%, to 46.17.
Even if earnings continue to beat forecasts, some analysts say a weak outlook for global growth and relatively
high stock valuations in the U.S. could make it hard for the market to move much higher.
"There's very little clarity on the economy outlook," said Abi Oladimeji, chief strategist at Thomas Miller
Investment. "That breeds uncertainty, and uncertainty breeds volatility."
The Stoxx Europe 600 climbed 0.3% to finish higher for five consecutive sessions.
Earlier, stocks in Japan had their strongest day in more than a month after Bank of Japan Gov. Haruhiko
Kuroda said the bank was ready to expand its bond-buying program and push interest rates further into
negative territory if necessary. Early Friday, the Nikkei was down 0.3%.
Chinese stocks were flat after the government reported that growth in China's economy slowed to 6.7% in the
first quarter from 6.8% the previous quarter. The Shanghai Composite was flat at 11 a.m.

Banks' Outlook Suffers As Rates Remain Low
Banks' Outlook Suffers As Rates Remain Low
By Aaron Kuriloff in New York and Riva Gold in London
1,048 words
11 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Bank stocks are having a terrible 2016, as central-bank policies, which for years lifted asset prices, are
hurting the financial sector.
The impact of economic stimulus efforts on lenders will get a fresh airing this week, as big U.S. banks begin
reporting their earnings for the first quarter. Trading revenue is expected to have taken a hit, but the more
enduring problem will be visible in the lenders' net interest margins, the basic measure of bank profitability
that gets flattened by low interest rates.
The broader U.S. stock market has shaken off a steep slump at the beginning of the year and is back in
positive territory. But banks and other financial companies are lagging far behind.
The divergence highlights the dilemma facing central banks. Easy-money policies have fueled a rally in risky
assets. They are also squeezing profits at financial companies, inflicting pain on a sector that is fundamental
to the health of the economy.
Earnings for S&P 500 financial companies during the quarter ended March are expected to be down 8.5%
from the same period last year, according to FactSet. Analysts have cut their projections for almost
three-quarters of the companies in the financial sector, including J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. and Bank of
America Corp., both of which are scheduled to release earnings this week.
"At the end of the day, it's just a more difficult earnings environment for financials," said Jeremy Zirin, head of
equity strategy at UBS Wealth Management Americas, which manages about $1 trillion.
Bank shares face an array of challenges. Low and in some cases negative interest rates globally have
eroded banks' earnings, while a steep decline in commodity prices at the start of the year raised concerns
over lenders' exposure to soured loans in the energy and mining sectors. In Europe, the picture has been
complicated further by concerns over nonperforming loans broadly, particularly in economically stressed
Southern Europe.
Financial companies are the worst performers in the S&P 500, down 7.6% in 2016 as the broader index has
risen 0.2%. The KBW Nasdaq Bank Index of large U.S. commercial lenders has fallen 15% this year. â€‹
Some big banks have done even worse. Morgan Stanley has fallen 25% this year, giving up roughly three
years of stock-price gains in the process. Bank of America is in a similar position, with its shares down 23%.
Citigroup Inc.'s shares have dropped 22%.
The situation is worse in Europe and Japan, where some investors say negative interest rates are acting as
a tax on banks because lenders essentially pay to park their deposits at the central bank.
While the MSCI World Index has pared its losses to less than 2% this year, the MSCI World Banks Index is
down 15%. Banks in Japan's Topix are down 34%. In the Stoxx Europe 600, bank shares have fallen 25%.
European bank shares are now trading at 2012 levels.
Bank stocks rallied earlier as the Federal Reserve raised interest rates in December for the first time in
nearly a decade. But investors dialed back the celebration as the European Central Bank moved deeper into
negative rates and the Bank of Japan adopted them, too.
While better-than-expected economic data in the U.S. have eased fears of a recession, the Fed has reduced
its expectations for the pace of interest-rate increases.
Rising rates are good for banks because they increase the gap between what banks charge on loans and
what they pay for deposits, a spread known as the net interest margin. Net interest margins at insured U.S.

--- Page 190 ---
Page 190 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
banks and thrifts fell to 3.13% on Dec. 31 from 3.69% at the end of 2010, according to the Federal Deposit
Insurance Corp.
European banks' price-to-earnings ratios relative to the MSCI Europe index are lower than at any point during
the global financial crisis, and their relative price-to-book values are close to all-time lows, according to
analysts at Morgan Stanley. Deutsche Bank AG trades at 31% of book value versus a five-year average of
57% at the end of December, according to FactSet. France's BNP Paribas SA trades at just 59% of book
value, compared with a five-year average of 73%.
J.P. Morgan Chase, the biggest U.S. bank by assets, trades at a slight discount to book. Citigroup and Bank
of America trade at far steeper discounts, just below 60% of book value. That is below their five-year
averages.
U.S. financial stocks are cheaper than their historical averages. The sector's trailing price/earnings ratio was
11.8 late last week, down from an average of 15 over the past 10 years, according to FactSet.
"You need people to look a year or two out and make the bet that we're going to get some better inflation
data and a stronger economy," said R.J. Grant, associate director of equity trading at KBW Inc. "And we're
just not seeing people believing that story right now."
Some investors said the worries have gone too far.
Michael Mattioli, portfolio manager for John Hancock Asset Management, which manages about $301 billion,
said he has been selectively adding to his bank holdings, focusing on the biggest U.S. banks.
"When you look at their balance sheets and from a capital or liquidity standpoint, the large-cap U.S. banks
have never been in a better position," he said.
Phil Poole, head of research at Deutsche Asset Management, which manages about 777 billion euros ($886
billion), said he is selectively buying bank credit, but he is being more selective about bank stocks right now
given questions around regulation in Europe as well as concerns over energy exposure.
Some investors said the rout in financial shares raises concerns about the sustainability of the rally in the
broader market since mid-February lows.
"I find it hard to grasp a continued rally without the participation of the second-largest group in the S&P 500,"
said Omar Aguilar, chief investment officer for equities at Charles Schwab Investment Management, which
oversees about $278 billion.

One U.S. Rate That's Already Gone Negative
By Min Zeng

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Negative interest rates have swept the globe, from Switzerland to Sweden to Japan.
By one measure, they are in the U.S., too.
The 2016 rally in government bond prices has taken U.S. real yields, which subtract inflation from the
10-year Treasury yield, below zero for the first time since 2012. When bond yields fall, their prices rise.
The 10-year U.S. Treasury yield settled at 1.722% Friday, which is below the latest reading on the core
consumer-price index of 2.3%. By this metric, the real U.S. 10-year yield is negative-0.58%.
Inflation is the main threat to bondholders. Many look at real yields because they reflect the real purchasing
powers investors obtain from investing in fixed-income assets. The fact that investors are willing to buy the
10-year note without enough compensation for an uptick in consumer prices has been confounding many
analysts. Some are concerned that this leaves the bond market vulnerable to jolts if sentiment sours.
About a quarter of government bonds in Japan and Europe have nominal yields below zero, reflecting policy
makers' embrace of negative rates in their latest bid to reflate stagnant economies.
Germany's 10-year bond is yielding below 0.1%, raising the prospect that it, too, may soon trade at negative
rates. The European Central Bank has increased its purchases, intensifying the investor scramble for safe
bonds.
Though negative nominal interest rates don't appear imminent in the U.S., investors say the gravitational pull
of low rates overseas stands to pull real yields here further into negative territory, by pushing down nominal
yields.
Guy Haselmann, head of U.S. interest-rate strategy at Bank of Nova Scotia, expects the 10-year nominal
yield to fall to its lowest level ever, 1.25%, before 2016 ends, even if there isn't another market shock that
leads to a retest of the February lows in stocks, riskier bonds and U.S. yields. The previous low, 1.40%, was
set at the height of the 2012 euro crisis.
"If things get really bad . . . the yield will test sub-1%," he said.
Such a state of affairs would have been unthinkable just a few years ago, when Wall Street analysts routinely
predicted a swift return to 5% long-term Treasury yields, let alone in the early 1980s, when yields briefly
topped 15% at the height of the U.S. inflation scare. That episode looms large in the minds of many
investors and, some analysts say, policy makers. With yields as low as they are, it doesn't take much
inflation to wipe out a bondholder's purchasing power, they say.
U.S. inflation has been running below the Federal Reserve's 2% medium-term target for years. Even so,
long-term U.S. real yields' foray into negative territory this year marks the first time since the euro crisis.
To be sure, U.S. real yields aren't negative at 10 years by every measure. One popular yardstick to measure
inflation-adjusted returns is the yield on the 10-year Treasuryinflation security. The yield was 0.126%
Friday, near the lowest level since April 2015, according to Tradeweb. It was 0.717% at the end of 2015.
Even so, the declines help explain much of what is going on in markets now, with stocks flat to slightly up for
the year and gold posting a 17% gain for 2016, reversing several years of declines.

Stocks Post Weekly Losses; Crude Surges
Friday's Markets
Stocks Post Weekly Losses; Crude Surges
By Corrie Driebusch and Riva Gold
641 words
9 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Rising oil prices buoyed stocks Friday, but major U.S. indexes still posted weekly losses in a rocky stretch.
Stocks around the globe declined this past week, as worries resurfaced about the ability of central bankers to
lift a sluggish global economy after years of easy monetary policy. It was the second week of declines in
three weeks for the major U.S. stock indexes.
The choppiness in the markets follows a rally that started in mid-February, when stocks posted gains of at
least 1% every week for more than a month.
"In many ways, the market came roaring back because the recession story was not validated," said Andrew
Slimmon, portfolio manager with Morgan Stanley Investment Management. "Having said that, now we're at a
juncture."
He said that in order for stocks to climb, there needs to be signs of solid earnings growth, and
better-than-expected guidance from companies as they report first-quarter results could provide this boost.
Investors will get a glimpse in the coming week as several notable companies, including major financial firms,
are set to release first-quarter results.
As of March 31, first-quarter earnings for S&P 500 companies are forecast to contract 8.5% from the
year-earlier period, which would mark a fourth consecutive quarterly decline, according to FactSet. Analysts
expect quarterly earnings to rise in the second half of the year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average added 35 points, or 0.2%, Friday to 17576.96 after earlier rising as much
as 153 points. The S&P 500 rose 5.69, or 0.3%, to 2047.60, giving it a gain of 0.2% so far this year.
The Dow industrials and the S&P 500 both posted weekly losses of 1.2%. The Nasdaq fell 1.3% for the week
and is still down 3.1% in 2016.
Big gains in the price of oil spurred the stock market's early gains on Friday, as U.S.-traded crude oil jumped
6.6% to $39.72 a barrel, bringing its weekly gain to 8%.
Energy companies in the S&P 500 advanced 2% on Friday, the best-performing sector in the index.
"As oil has come off its lows, the market has been trading on that," said Stephen Carl, head equity trader at
Williams Capital Group.
One of the biggest decliners was the consumer-discretionary sector, which was weighed down by retail
apparel companies. After the market closed Thursday, Gap reported that sinking sales would pressure profit
margins in its current quarter. Gap's stock slid $3.83, or 14%, to $23.85 Friday.
On Thursday, stocks in the U.S. pulled back, notching the Dow's biggest drop since late February. Late
Thursday, Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen and three former Fed leaders sought to dispel worries
about the U.S. economy while emphasizing a gradual path for interest rates.
"I don't think December was a mistake," Ms. Yellen said, referring to the central bank's interest-rate
increase. "We remain on a reasonable path."
The yield on the 10-year Treasury rose to 1.722% Friday, from 1.689% Thursday, but notched its second
consecutive week of declines. Yields rise when prices fall.
The Stoxx Europe 600 index rose on Friday but posted a 0.4% weekly decline, its fourth in a row. During that
period, the index fell 3%, putting its year-to-date decline at 9.3%.

Page 193 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Japanese yen, which tends to rise in times of market stress, rose slightly Friday, hitting its strongest level
against the dollar since late October 2014. The dollar dropped 0.1% against the yen to 108.06 yen.
Japan's Nikkei Stock Average rose 0.5% Friday but fell 2.1% for the week.

Stocks Fall, Bonds Rise in Rush to Safety --- Investors question strength of share rally since February lows, central banks' influence
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Fall, Bonds Rise in Rush to Safety --- Investors question strength of share rally since February
lows, central banks' influence
By Aaron Kuriloff and Min Zeng
613 words
8 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks slid, the yen strengthened and U.S. government bonds rose in a broad pullback from risk.
The gain in traditional havens and the Dow's biggest drop since Feb. 23 cast doubt on the strength of a rally
that has sent major U.S. stock indexes up roughly 12% from their Feb. 11 lows.
"We're just in a market where there's going to be heightened volatility," said Tony DeSpirito, portfolio
manager of the BlackRock Equity Dividend Fund.
Analysts said the continued rise in the Japanese currency has undermined investors' confidence in central
banks' abilities to boost growth, at a time when investors are concerned about sluggish economies around the
world.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell as much as 232 points before ending down 174.09, or 1%, at
17541.96. The S&P 500 fell 24.75, or 1.2%, to 2041.91 and the Nasdaq Composite declined 72.35, or 1.5%,
to 4848.37.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note fell to 1.689%, its lowest level since Feb. 11, compared with 1.753%
Wednesday. Bond prices rise as yields drop.
Gold for April delivery rose 1.1% to $1,236.20 an ounce. The metal is up 17% so far this year.
"It is risk-off big time," said Andrew Brenner, head of international fixed income for broker dealer National
Alliance Capital Markets.
The dollar fell 1.4% against Japan's yen to 108.21 yen, its lowest late New York level since October 2014 and
bringing its decline to 10% so far this year. The yen's resilience has surprised many investors since the Bank
of Japan adopted negative interest rates earlier this year, a measure that should have made the currency
less appealing for buyers.
The European Central Bank has faced a similar problem -- a strengthening currency despite ramped-up
stimulus efforts. On Thursday, ECB officials reiterated their willingness to launch new measures if needed to
fight low inflation.
In the U.S., minutes from the Federal Reserve's March policy meeting, released Wednesday, showed officials
were unlikely to raise interest rates in April.
"What we have here is a big case of uncertainty about what's next for monetary policy and the most urgent
case is in Japan," said Brian Jacobsen, chief portfolio strategist at Wells Fargo Funds Management.
Financial shares posted the biggest drops in the S&P 500, falling 1.9%, with some analysts saying a slower
pace of interest-rate increases threatens to squeeze bank earnings.
The KBW Nasdaq Bank Index of large U.S. commercial lenders lost 2.9%, while Citigroup fell $1.59, or 3.8%,
to $40.27 and Bank of America declined 42 cents, or 3.2%, to 12.85.
The Stoxx Europe 600 lost 0.8%, weighed down by a fall in banking shares, which shed 2.2%.
Stocks also fell In Asia early Friday. Japan's Nikkei was down 1.3%, South Korea's Kospi was down 0.9%,
Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index was down 0.7%, the Shanghai Composite was down 0.6% and Australia's
S&P ASX 200 was down 0.4%.

--- Page 195 ---
Page 195 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
On Thursday, U.S. crude oil lost 1.3% to $37.26 a barrel as doubts persisted that major oil producers would
agree to reduce their output.
Some investors said that while earnings are expected to be weak in the first quarter, a stabilization in energy
prices and the dollar could improve corporate outlooks for later in the year.

Health-Care Sector Boosts Stocks
Wednesday's Markets
Health-Care Sector Boosts Stocks
By Saumya Vaishampayan and Riva Gold
514 words
7 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks snapped a two-day losing streak, led higher by health-care shares.
Stocks have wavered in recent sessions after surging from Feb. 11 lows. With few major economic releases
or updates on U.S. monetary policy on the horizon, traders say the next move in the market is likely to be
sparked by first-quarter earnings.
"Earnings are the fulcrum right now," said Michael Purves, chief global strategist at Weeden & Co.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average added 112.73 points, or 0.6%, to 17716.05. The S&P 500 gained 21.49, or
1.1%, to 2066.66. The Nasdaq Composite rose 76.78, or 1.6%, to 4920.72.
Health-care shares in the S&P 500, which have lagged behind the broader market so far this year, rose 2.6%.
Pfizer and Allergan were among the gainers after they terminated their planned $150 billion merger. Pfizer
shares rose $1.57, or 5%, to $32.93 -- its largest one-day percentage rise since 2011. Allergan added 8.19,
or 3.5%, to 244.74.
Biotechnology stocks notched some of the biggest gains, with Vertex Pharmaceuticals rising 7.15, or 8.5%, to
91.31 and Celgene advancing 6.10, or 6%, to 108.22. The Nasdaq Biotechnology Index surged 6%, paring its
losses for the year to 16%.
"A sector that had been left for dead for the better part of this year, biotech, all of a sudden came to life," said
Eric Shenker, head of U.S. equity trading at Mizuho Securities USA.
Energy stocks posted the second-biggest advance in the S&P 500, bolstered by a steep rise in oil prices.
U.S. crude oil rose 5.2% to $37.75 a barrel after data showed U.S. crude supplies dropped last week.
Minutes from the Federal Reserve's March policy meeting, released Wednesday, showed officials leaning
against an April interest-rate increase.
"With the Federal Reserve backing off their expectations for the number of times they'll raise rates, you're
likely going to see investors reaching for yield," said Thomas Wilson, senior investment manager at Brinker
Capital. Mr. Wilson said he has added exposure to stocks that pay out high dividends, betting that those
stocks can continue to rally.
The dollar fell against the Japanese yen again, losing 0.5% to 109.80 yen.
Gold for April delivery fell 0.5% to $1,222.50 an ounce. The 10-year Treasury yield rose to 1.753%,
compared with 1.727% on Tuesday.
The Stoxx Europe 600 rose 0.8%. Stocks were mostly higher early Thursday in the Asia/Pacific region. Both
Japan's Nikkei and Australia's S&P ASX 200 were up 0.7%, while Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index was up
0.4%. The Shanghai Composite and South Korea's Kospi were flat.
"Concerns around China are subsiding a little bit, but that doesn't mean we're there yet," said Lukas Daalder,
chief investment officer at Robeco Investment Solutions.

Economic Jitters Send Stock Indexes Lower
Tuesday's Markets
Economic Jitters Send Stock Indexes Lower
By Leslie Josephs and Riva Gold
551 words
6 April 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks posted their biggest declines in weeks as investors grew cautious about the global economic
outlook.
Every sector in the S&P 500 fell, all but erasing the index's gains for the year. Assets perceived as havens --
such as gold, U.S. and German government bonds and the Japanese yen -- rose.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 133.68 points, or 0.8%, to 17603.32, its biggest loss since Feb. 23.
The S&P 500 lost 20.96 points, or 1%, to 2045.17, the index's biggest drop in four weeks and its first move of
at least 1% since March 11. Tuesday's session narrowed the S&P 500's 2016 gains to less than 0.1%. The
Nasdaq Composite fell 47.86 points, or 1%, Tuesday to 4843.93.
The S&P 500 is up 12% since hitting a nearly two-year low in February. But investors are now facing what is
expected to be a gloomy corporate-earnings season and oil prices that are still historically low.
The recent stock-market rally was "just excessive optimism," said Ilya Feygin, managing director at broker
WallachBeth Capital LLC.
Gold for April delivery rose 0.9% to $1,228.40 an ounce, while the yield on the 10-year Treasury note fell to
1.727%, compared with 1.779% Monday; bond prices rise when yields fall. The dollar fell 0.9% against the
yen to 110.34 yen.
The S&P 500's utilities sector, one of the year's biggest gainers, led declines, falling 1.9%.
Financial stocks in the broader index fell 1.4%. Persistent low interest rates can hurt banks' profitability
because it limits the amount they can charge on loans compared with what they pay for deposits.
Bank stocks have "been the whipping post" of the market, said Bill Nichols, head of U.S. equities at Cantor
Fitzgerald. Bank of America fell 32 cents, or 2.4%, to $13.19, while Morgan Stanley lost 66 cents, or 2.6%, to
24.38.
Allergan fell $41, or 15%, to $236.55, making it the biggest laggard in the S&P 500, after the Treasury
Department on Monday imposed new rules designed to curb corporate inversions, casting doubt on the Botox
maker's proposed merger with Pfizer. Pfizer rose 64 cents, or 2.1% to 31.36. Health-care shares in the S&P
500 lost 1.2%.
Economic data stoked concerns about the health of the global economy Tuesday.
The U.S. trade deficit in February hit its highest level since August, the Commerce Department said. The
eurozone economy grew at a weaker pace last month than earlier estimated by a survey of purchasing
managers, according to data firm Markit. German manufacturing orders unexpectedly declined in February
from the preceding month, the country's economics ministry said.
The Stoxx Europe 600 fell 1.9% to its lowest level since Feb. 25, while Germany's DAX fell 2.6%, its biggest
percentage decline since Feb. 24.
Asian stocks were slightly higher Wednesday at midday. Japan's Nikkei was up 0.3%, Australia's S&P ASX
200 was up 0.6% and Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index was up 0.2%.
